The **Sun** is the [star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star "Star") at the centre of the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"). It is a massive, nearly perfect sphere of hot [plasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasma_(physics) "Plasma (physics)"), heated to [incandescence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incandescence "Incandescence") by [nuclear fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_fusion "Nuclear fusion") reactions in its core, radiating the energy from its [surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface "Surface") mainly as [visible light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_light "Visible light") and [infrared radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared_radiation "Infrared radiation") with 10% at [ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet") energies. It is by far the most important source of energy for [life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life") on [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth"). The Sun has been an [object of veneration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Sun_in_culture "The Sun in culture") in many cultures and a central subject for astronomical research since [antiquity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_history "Ancient history").

The Sun orbits the [Galactic Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center") at a distance of 24,000 to 28,000 [light-years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year "Light-year"). Its distance from Earth defines the [astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit"), which is about 1.496×10 8 kilometres or about 8 [light-minutes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-minute "Light-minute"). [Its diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radius "Solar radius") is about 1,391,400 km (864,600 mi), 109 times that of Earth. [The Sun's mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass") is about 330,000 times that of Earth, making up about 99.86% of the total mass of the Solar System. The mass of outer layer of the Sun's atmosphere, its _photosphere_, consists mostly of [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") (~73%) and [helium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium "Helium") (~25%), with much smaller quantities of heavier elements, including [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), [neon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neon "Neon"), and [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron").

The Sun is a [G-type main-sequence star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star "G-type main-sequence star") (G2V), informally called a yellow dwarf, though its light is actually white. It formed approximately 4.6 billion[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-short-17) years ago from the [gravitational collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_collapse "Gravitational collapse") of matter within a region of a large [molecular cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_cloud "Molecular cloud"). Most of this matter gathered in the centre; the rest flattened into an orbiting disk that [became the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and evolution of the Solar System"). The central mass became so hot and dense that it eventually initiated nuclear fusion in [its core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_core "Solar core"). Every second, the Sun's core fuses about 600 billion [kilograms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilograms "Kilograms") (kg) of hydrogen into helium and converts 4 billion kg of [matter into energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence "Mass–energy equivalence").

About 4 to 7 billion years from now, when [hydrogen fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_fusion "Hydrogen fusion") in the Sun's core diminishes to the point where the Sun is no longer in [hydrostatic equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium"), its core will undergo a marked increase in density and temperature which will cause its outer layers to expand, eventually transforming the Sun into a [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant"). After the red giant phase, models suggest the Sun will shed its outer layers and become a dense type of cooling star (a [white dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_dwarf "White dwarf")), and no longer produce energy by fusion, but will still glow and give off heat from its previous fusion for perhaps trillions of years. After that, it is theorised to become a super dense [black dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_dwarf "Black dwarf"), giving off negligible energy.

Etymology
---------

The English word _sun_ developed from [Old English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English "Old English")_sunne_. Cognates appear in other [Germanic languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_languages "Germanic languages"), including [West Frisian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Frisian_language "West Frisian language")_sinne_, [Dutch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_language "Dutch language")_zon_, [Low German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_German "Low German")_Sünn_, [Standard German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_German "Standard German")_Sonne_, [Bavarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bavarian_language "Bavarian language")_Sunna_, [Old Norse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_Norse "Old Norse")_sunna_, and [Gothic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gothic_language "Gothic language")_sunnō_. All these words stem from [Proto-Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Germanic "Proto-Germanic")*_sunnōn_.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-BARNHART776-18)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Orel-19) This is ultimately related to the word for _sun_ in other branches of the [Indo-European language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-European_language "Indo-European language") family, though in most cases a [nominative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nominative "Nominative") stem with an _l_ is found, rather than the [genitive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genitive "Genitive") stem in _n_, as for example in [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin")_sōl_, [ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek")ἥλιος (_hēlios_), [Welsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welsh_language "Welsh language")_haul_ and [Czech](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czech_language "Czech language")_slunce_, as well as (with *l >_r_) Sanskrit स्वर् (_svár_) and [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language")خور (_xvar_). Indeed, the _l_-stem survived in Proto-Germanic as well, as *_sōwelan_, which gave rise to Gothic _sauil_ (alongside _sunnō_) and [Old Norse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_Norse "Old Norse") prosaic _sól_ (alongside poetic _sunna_), and through it the words for _sun_ in the modern Scandinavian languages: [Swedish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swedish_language "Swedish language") and [Danish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Danish_language "Danish language")_sol_, [Icelandic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Icelandic_language "Icelandic language")_sól_, etc.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Orel-19)

The principal adjectives for the Sun in English are _sunny_ for sunlight and, in technical contexts, _solar_ (),[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-OED2-3) from Latin _sol_.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-20) From the Greek _helios_ comes the rare adjective _heliac_ ().[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-21) In English, the Greek and Latin words occur in poetry as personifications of the Sun, [Helios](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helios "Helios") () and [Sol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sol_(Roman_mythology) "Sol (Roman mythology)") (),[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Lexico-2)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-OED-1) while in science fiction _Sol_ may be used to distinguish the Sun from other stars. The term _[sol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sol\_(day\_on\_Mars) "Sol (day on Mars)")_ with a lowercase _s_ is used by planetary astronomers for the duration of a [solar day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_day "Solar day") on another planet such as [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars").[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-22)

The [astronomical symbol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_symbol "Astronomical symbol") for the Sun is a circle with a central dot, ☉.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-23) It is used for such units as _M_☉ ([Solar mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass")), _R_☉ ([Solar radius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radius "Solar radius")) and _L_☉ ([Solar luminosity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_luminosity "Solar luminosity")).[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-24)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-25) The scientific study of the Sun is called _heliology_.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-26)

General characteristics
-----------------------

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Solar_System_true_color_%28title_and_caption%29.jpg/500px-Solar_System_true_color_%28title_and_caption%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Solar_System_true_color_(title_and_caption).jpg)

Size comparison of major celestial objects in the Solar System, including the Sun

The Sun is a [G-type main-sequence star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star "G-type main-sequence star") that makes up about 99.86% of the mass of the Solar System.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Woolfson00-27) It has an [absolute magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_magnitude "Absolute magnitude") of +4.83, estimated to be brighter than about 85% of the stars in the [Milky Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way "Milky Way"), most of which are [red dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf").[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-28)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-29) It is more massive than 95% of the stars within 7 pc (23 ly).[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-30) The Sun is a [Population I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_I_stars "Population I stars"), or heavy-element-rich,[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-heavy_elements-31) star.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-zeilik-32) Its formation approximately 4.6 billion years ago may have been triggered by shockwaves from one or more nearby [supernovae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova "Supernova").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Connelly2012-33)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Falk-34) This is suggested by a high [abundance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements "Abundance of the chemical elements") of heavy elements in the Solar System, such as [gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold "Gold") and [uranium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranium "Uranium"), relative to the abundances of these elements in so-called [Population II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_II "Population II"), heavy-element-poor, stars. The heavy elements could most plausibly have been produced by [endothermic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothermic "Endothermic") nuclear reactions during a supernova, or by [transmutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation "Nuclear transmutation") through [neutron absorption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_absorption "Neutron absorption") within a massive second-generation star.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-zeilik-32)

The Sun is by far the [brightest object in the Earth's sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_brightest_natural_objects_in_the_sky "List of brightest natural objects in the sky"), with an [apparent magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent magnitude") of −26.74.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-35)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-36) This is about 13 billion times brighter than the next brightest star, [Sirius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sirius "Sirius"), which has an apparent magnitude of −1.46.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Hoffleit1991-37)

One [astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit") (about 150 million kilometres; 93 million miles) is defined as the mean distance between the centres of the Sun and the Earth. The instantaneous distance varies by about ±2.5 million kilometres (1.6 million miles) as Earth moves from [perihelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion "Perihelion") around 3 January to [aphelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphelion "Aphelion") around 4 July.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-USNO-38) At its average distance, light travels from the Sun's horizon to Earth's horizon in about 8 minutes and 20 seconds,[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-39) while light from the closest points of the Sun and Earth takes about two seconds less. The energy of this [sunlight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunlight "Sunlight") supports almost all life[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-40) on Earth by [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis"),[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-41) and drives [Earth's climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_climate "Earth's climate") and weather.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-42)

The Sun does not have a definite boundary, but its density decreases exponentially with increasing height above the [photosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosphere "Photosphere").[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Beer_et_al,_2012-41-43) For the purpose of measurement, the Sun's radius is considered to be the distance from its centre to the edge of the photosphere, the apparent visible surface of the Sun.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-73-44) The roundness of the Sun is the relative difference between its radius at its equator, ![Image 4: {\displaystyle R_{\textrm {eq}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8e8fb785c8630b31c4161720ca451e18f8c1a43e), and at its pole, ![Image 5: {\displaystyle R_{\textrm {pol}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cacaa4fdf7ee7280b62021eaad0749e8d4063a55), called the [oblateness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oblateness "Oblateness"),[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Meftah-2015-45)

![Image 6: {\displaystyle \Delta _{\odot }=(R_{\textrm {eq}}-R_{\textrm {pol}})/R_{\textrm {pol}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1d7c549c5d87bf482b4f10f78737b6ae11febcfc)

The value is difficult to measure. Atmospheric distortion means the measurement must be done on satellites; the value is very small meaning very precise technique is needed.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-GoughOverview2012-46)

The oblateness was once proposed to be sufficient to explain the [perihelion precession of Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion_precession_of_Mercury "Perihelion precession of Mercury") but Einstein proposed that [general relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_relativity "General relativity") could explain the precession using a spherical Sun.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-GoughOverview2012-46) When high precision measurements of the oblateness became available via the [Solar Dynamics Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_Dynamics_Observatory "Solar Dynamics Observatory")[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-47) and the [Picard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Picard_(satellite) "Picard (satellite)") satellite[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Meftah-2015-45) the measured value was even smaller than expected,[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-GoughOverview2012-46)8.2×10−6, or 8 parts per million. These measurements determined the Sun to be the natural object closest to a perfect sphere ever observed.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-perfect_sphere-48) The oblateness value remains constant independent of solar irradiation changes.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Meftah-2015-45) The tidal effect of the planets is weak and does not significantly affect the shape of the Sun.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Schutz2003-49)

### Rotation

The Sun rotates faster at its equator than at its [poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies "Poles of astronomical bodies"). This [differential rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_rotation "Solar rotation") is caused by [convective motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convection "Convection") due to heat transport and the [Coriolis force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coriolis_effect "Coriolis effect") due to the Sun's rotation. In a frame of reference defined by the stars, the rotational period is approximately 25.6 days at the equator and 33.5 days at the poles. Viewed from Earth as it orbits the Sun, the apparent rotational period of the Sun at its equator is about 28 days.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-78-50) Viewed from a vantage point above its north pole, the Sun rotates [counterclockwise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterclockwise "Counterclockwise") around its axis of spin.[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-rotation-51)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-spaceacademy-52)

A survey of [solar analogues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_analog "Solar analog") suggests the early Sun was rotating up to ten times faster than it does today. This would have made the surface much more active, with greater X-ray and UV emission. [Sunspots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunspot "Sunspot") would have covered 5–30% of the surface.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-53) The rotation rate was gradually slowed by [magnetic braking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_braking_(astronomy) "Magnetic braking (astronomy)"), as the Sun's magnetic field interacted with the outflowing [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind").[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-54) A vestige of this rapid primordial rotation still survives at the Sun's core, which rotates at a rate of once per week; four times the mean surface rotation rate.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-55)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-56)

Composition
-----------

The Sun consists mainly of the elements [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") and [helium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium "Helium"). At this time in the Sun's life, they account for 74.9% and 23.8%, respectively, of the mass of the Sun in the photosphere.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-lodders-57) All heavier elements, called _[metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metallicity "Metallicity")_ in astronomy, account for less than 2% of the mass, with [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") (roughly 1% of the Sun's mass), [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon") (0.3%), [neon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neon "Neon") (0.2%), and [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron") (0.2%) being the most abundant.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-hkt2004-58)

The Sun's original chemical composition was inherited from the [interstellar medium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_medium "Interstellar medium") out of which it formed. Originally it would have been about 71.1% hydrogen, 27.4% helium, and 1.5% heavier elements.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-lodders-57) The hydrogen and most of the helium in the Sun would have been produced by [Big Bang nucleosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis "Big Bang nucleosynthesis") in the first 20 minutes of the universe, and the heavier elements were [produced by previous generations of stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis "Stellar nucleosynthesis") before the Sun was formed, and spread into the interstellar medium during the [final stages of stellar life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_evolution "Stellar evolution") and by events such as [supernovae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova "Supernova").[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-hkt2004_78-59)

Since the Sun formed, the main fusion process has involved fusing hydrogen into helium. Over the past 4.6 billion years, the amount of helium and its location within the Sun has gradually changed. The proportion of helium within the core has increased from about 24% to about 60% due to fusion, and some of the helium and heavy elements have settled from the photosphere toward the centre of the Sun because of [gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity "Gravity"). The proportions of heavier elements are unchanged. [Heat is transferred](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_transfer "Heat transfer") outward from the Sun's core by radiation rather than by convection (see [Radiative zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#Radiative_zone) below), so the fusion products are not lifted outward by heat; they remain in the core,[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-hkt2004_9.2.3-60) and gradually an inner core of helium has begun to form that cannot be fused because presently the Sun's core is not hot or dense enough to fuse helium. In the current photosphere, the helium fraction is reduced, and the [metallicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metallicity "Metallicity") is only 84% of what it was in the [protostellar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protostar "Protostar") phase (before nuclear fusion in the core started). In the future, helium will continue to accumulate in the core, and in about 5 billion years this gradual build-up will eventually cause the Sun to exit the [main sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Main_sequence "Main sequence") and become a [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant").[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-61)

The chemical composition of the photosphere is normally considered representative of the composition of the primordial Solar System.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-aller1968-62) Typically, the solar heavy-element abundances described above are measured both by using [spectroscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy "Astronomical spectroscopy") of the Sun's photosphere and by measuring abundances in [meteorites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorites "Meteorites") that have never been heated to melting temperatures. These meteorites are thought to retain the composition of the protostellar Sun and are thus not affected by the settling of heavy elements. The two methods generally agree well.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-basu2008-63)

Structure
---------

[![Image 7: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sun_poster.svg/960px-Sun_poster.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sun_poster.svg)

Illustration of the Sun's structure, in false colour for contrast

### Core

The core of the Sun extends from the centre to about 20–25% of the solar radius.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Garcia2007-64) It has a density of up to 150 g/cm 3[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Basu-65)[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66) (about 150 times the density of water) and a temperature of close to 15.7 million [kelvin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin "Kelvin") (K).[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66) By contrast, the Sun's surface temperature is about 5800 K. Recent analysis of [SOHO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory "Solar and Heliospheric Observatory") mission data favours the idea that the core is rotating faster than the radiative zone outside it.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Garcia2007-64) Through most of the Sun's life, energy has been produced by nuclear fusion in the core region through the [proton–proton chain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain "Proton–proton chain"); this process converts hydrogen into helium.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-67) Currently, 0.8% of the energy generated in the Sun comes from another sequence of fusion reactions called the [CNO cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CNO_cycle "CNO cycle"); the proportion coming from the CNO cycle is expected to increase as the Sun becomes older and more luminous.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-jpcs271_1_012031-68)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-69)

The core is the only region of the Sun that produces an appreciable amount of [thermal energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_energy "Thermal energy") through fusion; 99% of the Sun's power is generated in the innermost 24% of its radius, and almost no fusion occurs beyond 30% of the radius. The rest of the Sun is heated by this energy as it is transferred outward through many successive layers, finally to the solar photosphere where it escapes into space through radiation (photons) or advection (massive particles).[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-70)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Zirker2002-15-71)

[![Image 8: circles and arrows showing protons combining in a series of fusion reactions yielding helium-3 which breaks down tow helium-4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Proton-proton_reaction_chain.svg/250px-Proton-proton_reaction_chain.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Proton-proton_reaction_chain.svg)

Illustration of a proton-proton reaction chain, from hydrogen forming [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium "Deuterium"), [helium-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-3 "Helium-3"), and regular [helium-4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-4 "Helium-4")

The proton–proton chain occurs around 9.2×10 37 times each second in the core, converting about 3.7×10 38 protons into [alpha particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_particle "Alpha particle") (helium nuclei) every second (out of a total of ~8.9×10 56 free protons in the Sun), or about 6.2×10 11 kg/s. However, each proton (on average) takes around 9 billion years to fuse with another using the PP chain.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-70) Fusing four free [protons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton "Proton") (hydrogen nuclei) into a single alpha particle (helium nucleus) releases around 0.7% of the fused mass as energy,[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-72) so the Sun releases energy at the mass–energy conversion rate of 4.26 billion kg/s (which requires 600 billion kg of hydrogen[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-73)), for 384.6[yottawatts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yotta- "Yotta-") (3.846×10 26 W),[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-nssdc-5) or 9.192×10 10[megatons of TNT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TNT_equivalent "TNT equivalent") per second. The large power output of the Sun is mainly due to the huge size and density of its core (compared to Earth and objects on Earth), with only a fairly small amount of power being generated per [cubic metre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cubic_metre "Cubic metre"). Theoretical models of the Sun's interior indicate a maximum power density, or energy production, of approximately 276.5 [watts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Watt "Watt") per cubic metre at the centre of the core,[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-74) which, according to [Karl Kruszelnicki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Kruszelnicki "Karl Kruszelnicki"), is about the same power density inside a [compost pile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compost_pile "Compost pile").[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-75)

The fusion rate in the core is in a self-correcting equilibrium: a slightly higher rate of fusion would cause the core to heat up more and [expand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_expansion "Thermal expansion") slightly against the weight of the outer layers, reducing the density and hence the fusion rate and correcting the [perturbation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy) "Perturbation (astronomy)"); and a slightly lower rate would cause the core to cool and shrink slightly, increasing the density and increasing the fusion rate and again reverting it to its present rate.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-76)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-77)

### Radiative zone

[![Image 9: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Heat_Transfer_in_Stars.svg/330px-Heat_Transfer_in_Stars.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Heat_Transfer_in_Stars.svg)

Illustration of different stars' internal structure based on mass. The Sun in the middle has an inner radiating zone and an outer convective zone.

The radiative zone is the thickest layer of the Sun, at 0.45 solar radii. From the core out to about 0.7 [solar radii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radius "Solar radius"), [thermal radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_radiation "Thermal radiation") is the primary means of energy transfer.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-autogenerated1-78) The temperature drops from approximately 7 million to 2 million kelvins with increasing distance from the core.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66) This [temperature gradient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperature_gradient "Temperature gradient") is less than the value of the [adiabatic lapse rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate "Adiabatic lapse rate") and hence cannot drive convection, which explains why the transfer of energy through this zone is by [radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiation "Radiation") instead of thermal convection.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66)[Ions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ions "Ions") of hydrogen and helium emit photons, which travel only a brief distance before being reabsorbed by other ions.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-autogenerated1-78) The density drops a hundredfold (from 20,000 kg/m 3 to 200 kg/m 3) between 0.25 solar radii and 0.7 radii, the top of the radiative zone.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-autogenerated1-78)

### Tachocline

The radiative zone and the convective zone are separated by a transition layer, the [tachocline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tachocline "Tachocline"). This is a region where the sharp regime change between the uniform rotation of the radiative zone and the differential rotation of the [convection zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convection_zone "Convection zone") results in a large [shear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shear_(fluid) "Shear (fluid)") between the two—a condition where successive horizontal layers slide past one another.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-79) Presently, it is hypothesised that a magnetic dynamo, or [solar dynamo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_dynamo "Solar dynamo"), within this layer generates the Sun's [magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field").[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66)

### Convective zone

The Sun's convection zone extends from 0.7 solar radii (500,000 km) to near the surface. In this layer, the solar plasma is not dense or hot enough to transfer the heat energy of the interior outward via radiation. Instead, the density of the plasma is low enough to allow convective currents to develop and move the Sun's energy outward towards its surface. Material heated at the tachocline picks up heat and expands, thereby reducing its density and allowing it to rise. As a result, an orderly motion of the mass develops into thermal cells that carry most of the heat outward to the Sun's photosphere above. Once the material diffusively and radiatively cools just beneath the photospheric surface, its density increases, and it sinks to the base of the convection zone, where it again picks up heat from the top of the radiative zone and the convective cycle continues. At the photosphere, the temperature has dropped 350-fold to 5,700 K (9,800°F) and the density to only 0.2 g/m 3 (about 1/10,000 the density of air at sea level, and 1 millionth that of the inner layer of the convective zone).[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66)

The thermal columns of the convection zone form an imprint on the surface of the Sun giving it a granular appearance called the [solar granulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_granulation "Solar granulation") at the smallest scale and [supergranulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supergranulation "Supergranulation") at larger scales. Turbulent convection in this outer part of the solar interior sustains "small-scale" dynamo action over the near-surface volume of the Sun.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA1-66) The Sun's thermal columns are [Bénard cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B%C3%A9nard_cells "Bénard cells") and take the shape of roughly hexagonal prisms.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-80)

### Atmosphere

The solar atmosphere is the region of the Sun that extends from the top of the convection zone to the inner boundary of the [heliosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliosphere "Heliosphere"). It is often divided into three primary layers: the photosphere, the [chromosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromosphere "Chromosphere"), and the [corona](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_corona "Stellar corona").[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-81) The chromosphere and corona are separated by a thin [transition region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_transition_region "Solar transition region") that is frequently considered as an additional distinct layer.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-82): 173–174  Some sources consider the heliosphere to be the _outer_ or _extended solar atmosphere_.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-83)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-84)

#### Photosphere

[![Image 10: A false-colour image of the solar photosphere](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Highest_resolution_photo_of_Sun_%28NSF%29_as_of_January_20%2C_2020.jpg/250px-Highest_resolution_photo_of_Sun_%28NSF%29_as_of_January_20%2C_2020.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Highest_resolution_photo_of_Sun_(NSF)_as_of_January_20,_2020.jpg)

The photosphere is structured by convection cells referred to as _[granules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar\_granule "Solar granule")_.

The visible surface of the Sun, the photosphere, is the layer below which the Sun becomes [opaque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opacity_(optics) "Opacity (optics)") to visible light.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) Photons produced in this layer escape the Sun through the transparent solar atmosphere above it and become solar radiation, sunlight. The change in opacity is due to the decreasing amount of [H− ions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_anion "Hydrogen anion"), which absorb visible light easily.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) Conversely, the visible light perceived is produced as electrons react with hydrogen atoms to produce H− ions.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Gibson-86)[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Shu-87)

The photosphere is tens to hundreds of kilometres thick, and is slightly less opaque than air on Earth. Because the upper part of the photosphere is cooler than the lower part, an image of the Sun appears brighter in the centre than on the edge or _limb_ of the solar disk, in a phenomenon known as _[limb darkening](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limb\_darkening "Limb darkening")_.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) The spectrum of sunlight has approximately the spectrum of a [black-body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black-body "Black-body") radiating at 5,772 K (9,930°F),[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-IAU2015resB3-12) interspersed with atomic [absorption lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_line "Absorption line") from the tenuous layers above the photosphere. The photosphere has a particle density of ~10 23 m−3 (about 0.37% of the particle number per volume of [Earth's atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere "Earth's atmosphere") at sea level). The photosphere is not fully ionised—the extent of ionisation is about 3%, leaving almost all of the hydrogen in atomic form.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-88)

The coolest layer of the Sun is a temperature minimum region extending to about 500 km above the photosphere, and has a temperature of about 4,100 K.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) This part of the Sun is cool enough to allow for the existence of simple molecules such as [carbon monoxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_monoxide "Carbon monoxide") and water.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solanki1994-89)

#### Chromosphere

Above the temperature minimum layer is a layer about 2,000 km thick, dominated by a spectrum of emission and absorption lines.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) It is called the _chromosphere_ from the Greek root _chroma_, meaning colour, because the chromosphere is visible as a coloured flash at the beginning and end of total solar eclipses.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-autogenerated1-78) The temperature of the chromosphere increases gradually with altitude, ranging up to around 20,000 K near the top.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85) In the upper part of the chromosphere helium becomes partially [ionised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionization "Ionization").[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Hansteen1997-90)

[![Image 11: A photograph of the surface of the sun, with flares terminating from the surface on the left.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg/250px-171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg)

The Sun's transition region taken by [Hinode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinode_(satellite) "Hinode (satellite)")'s Solar Optical Telescope

The chromosphere and overlying corona are separated by a thin (about 200 km) transition region where the temperature rises rapidly from around 20,000 K in the upper chromosphere to coronal temperatures closer to 1,000,000 K.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91) The temperature increase is facilitated by the full ionisation of helium in the transition region, which significantly reduces radiative cooling of the plasma.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Hansteen1997-90) The transition region does not occur at a well-defined altitude, but forms a kind of nimbus around chromospheric features such as [spicules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_spicule "Solar spicule") and [filaments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_filament "Solar filament"), and is in constant, chaotic motion.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-autogenerated1-78) The transition region is not easily visible from Earth's surface, but is readily observable from space by instruments sensitive to [extreme ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_ultraviolet "Extreme ultraviolet").[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-92)

#### Corona

[![Image 12: A photograph of a solar eclipse](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/2017_Total_Solar_Eclipse_%2835909952653%29.jpg/250px-2017_Total_Solar_Eclipse_%2835909952653%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2017_Total_Solar_Eclipse_(35909952653).jpg)

During a total [solar eclipse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_eclipse "Solar eclipse") the solar corona can be seen with the naked eye.

The corona is the next layer of the Sun. The low corona, near the surface of the Sun, has a particle density around 10 15 m−3 to 10 16 m−3.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Hansteen1997-90)[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-particle_density-93) The average temperature of the corona and solar wind is about 1,000,000–2,000,000 K; however, in the hottest regions it is 8,000,000–20,000,000 K.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91) Although no complete theory yet exists to account for the temperature of the corona, at least some of its heat is known to be from [magnetic reconnection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection "Magnetic reconnection").[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91)[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Russell2001-94)

The outer boundary of the corona is located where the radially increasing, large-scale [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") speed is equal to the radially decreasing [Alfvén wave phase speed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave "Alfvén wave"). This defines a closed, nonspherical surface, referred to as the _[Alfvén critical surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n\_critical\_surface "Alfvén critical surface")_, below which coronal flows are [sub-Alfvénic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_Mach_number "Alfvén Mach number") and above which the solar wind is super-Alfvénic.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-95) The height at which this transition occurs varies across space and with solar activity, reaching its lowest near solar minimum and its highest near solar maximum. In April 2021 the surface was crossed for the first time at heliocentric distances ranging from 16 to 20 solar radii by the [Parker Solar Probe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parker_Solar_Probe "Parker Solar Probe").[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-96)[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-touching-97) Predictions of its full possible extent have placed its full range within 8 to 30 solar radii.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-98)[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-99)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-100)

### Heliosphere

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/PIA22835-VoyagerProgram%26Heliosphere-Chart-20181210.png/250px-PIA22835-VoyagerProgram%26Heliosphere-Chart-20181210.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA22835-VoyagerProgram%26Heliosphere-Chart-20181210.png)

Depiction of the [heliosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliosphere "Heliosphere")

The heliosphere is defined as the region of space where the solar wind dominates over the interstellar medium.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-101) Turbulence and dynamic forces in the heliosphere cannot affect the shape of the solar corona within, because the information can only travel at the speed of Alfvén waves. The solar wind travels outward continuously through the heliosphere,[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-102)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-103) forming the solar magnetic field into a [spiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parker_spiral "Parker spiral") shape,[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Russell2001-94) until it impacts the [heliopause](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy) "Heliopause (astronomy)") more than 50 AU from the Sun. In December 2004, the _[Voyager 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voyager\_1 "Voyager 1")_ probe passed through a shock front that is thought to be part of the heliopause.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-104) In late 2012, _Voyager 1_ recorded a marked increase in [cosmic ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_ray "Cosmic ray") collisions and a sharp drop in lower energy particles from the solar wind, which suggested that the probe had passed through the heliopause and entered the [interstellar medium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_medium "Interstellar medium"),[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-105) and indeed did so on 25 August 2012, at approximately 122 astronomical units (18 Tm) from the Sun.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-106) The heliosphere has a [heliotail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliosphere#Heliotail "Heliosphere") which stretches out behind it due to the Sun's [peculiar motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peculiar_motion "Peculiar motion") through the galaxy.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-107)

Solar radiation
---------------

[![Image 14: A photograph of the sun with a layer of fog visible in front of it.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sun_in_fog_in_Lysekil.jpg/250px-Sun_in_fog_in_Lysekil.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sun_in_fog_in_Lysekil.jpg)

The Sun seen through a light fog

The Sun emits light across the [visible spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum"). Its colour is [white](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White "White"), with a [CIE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIE_1931_color_space "CIE 1931 color space") colour-space index near (0.3, 0.3), when viewed from space or when the Sun is high in the sky. The Solar radiance per wavelength peaks in the green portion of the spectrum when viewed from space.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-108)[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-109) When the Sun is very low in the sky, [atmospheric scattering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffuse_sky_radiation "Diffuse sky radiation") renders the Sun yellow, red, orange, or magenta, and in rare occasions even [green or blue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_flash "Green flash"). Some cultures mentally picture the Sun as yellow and some even red; the cultural reasons for this are debated.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-yellow_sun_paradox-110) The Sun is classed as a _G2_ star,[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-70) meaning it is a [G-type star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-type_star "G-type star"), with _2_ indicating its [surface temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effective_temperature "Effective temperature") is in the second range of the G class.

The [solar constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_constant "Solar constant") is the amount of power that the Sun deposits per unit area that is directly exposed to sunlight. The solar constant is equal to approximately 1,368 W/m 2 (watts per square metre) at a distance of one [astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit") (AU) from the Sun (that is, at or near Earth's orbit).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-TSI-111) Sunlight on the surface of Earth is [attenuated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attenuation_(electromagnetic_radiation) "Attenuation (electromagnetic radiation)") by [Earth's atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth"), so that less power arrives at the surface (closer to 1,000 W/m 2) in clear conditions when the Sun is near the [zenith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zenith "Zenith").[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-El-Sharkawi2005-112) Sunlight at the top of Earth's atmosphere is composed (by total energy) of about 50% infrared light, 40% visible light, and 10% ultraviolet light.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_radiation-113) The atmosphere filters out over 70% of solar ultraviolet, especially at the shorter wavelengths.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-114) Solar [ultraviolet radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation "Ultraviolet radiation") ionises Earth's dayside upper atmosphere, creating its electrically conducting [ionosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionosphere "Ionosphere").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Phillips1995-115)

[Ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet") light from the Sun has [antiseptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiseptic "Antiseptic") properties and can be used to sanitise tools and water. This radiation causes [sunburn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunburn "Sunburn"), and has other biological effects such as the production of [vitamin D](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitamin_D "Vitamin D") and [sun tanning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_tanning "Sun tanning"). It is the main cause of [skin cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skin_cancer "Skin cancer"). Ultraviolet light is strongly attenuated by Earth's [ozone layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone_layer "Ozone layer"), so that the amount of UV varies greatly with [latitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") and has been partially responsible for many biological adaptations, including variations in [human skin colour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_skin_colour "Human skin colour").[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-116)

High-energy [gamma ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma_ray "Gamma ray")[photons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photon "Photon") initially released with fusion reactions in the core are almost immediately absorbed by the solar plasma of the radiative zone, usually after travelling only a few millimetres. Re-emission happens in a random direction and usually at slightly lower energy. With this sequence of emissions and absorptions, it takes a long time for radiation to reach the Sun's surface. Estimates of the photon travel time range between 10,000 and 170,000 years.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-NASA-117) In contrast, it takes only 2.3 seconds for [neutrinos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrino "Neutrino"), which account for about 2% of the total energy production of the Sun, to reach the surface. Because energy transport in the Sun is a process that involves photons in [thermodynamic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamics "Thermodynamics") equilibrium with [matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter "Matter"), the time scale of energy transport in the Sun is longer, on the order of 30,000,000 years. This is the time it would take the Sun to return to a stable state if the rate of energy generation in its core were suddenly changed.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-118)

[Electron neutrinos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_neutrino "Electron neutrino") are released by fusion reactions in the core, but, unlike photons, they rarely interact with matter, so almost all are able to escape the Sun immediately. However, measurements of the number of these neutrinos produced in the Sun are [lower than theories predict](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_neutrino_problem "Solar neutrino problem") by a factor of 3. In 2001, the discovery of [neutrino oscillation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation "Neutrino oscillation") resolved the discrepancy: the Sun emits the number of electron neutrinos predicted by the theory, but neutrino detectors were missing 2⁄3 of them because the neutrinos had changed [flavor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics) "Flavor (particle physics)") by the time they were detected.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Schlattl-119)

Magnetic activity
-----------------

The Sun has a [stellar magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_magnetic_field "Stellar magnetic field") that varies across its surface. Its polar field is 1–2 [gauss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_(unit) "Gauss (unit)") (0.0001–0.0002[T](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)")), whereas the field is typically 3,000 gauss (0.3 T) in features on the Sun called _sunspots_ and 10–100 gauss (0.001–0.01 T) in [solar prominences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_prominence "Solar prominence").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-nssdc-5) The magnetic field varies in time and location. The quasi-periodic 11-year [solar cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_cycle "Solar cycle") is the most prominent variation in which the number and size of sunspots waxes and wanes.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-doi10.1146/annurev-astro-081913-040012-120)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Zirker2002-119-121)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Lang-122)

The solar magnetic field extends well beyond the Sun itself. The electrically conducting solar wind plasma carries the Sun's magnetic field into space, forming what is called the [interplanetary magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interplanetary_magnetic_field "Interplanetary magnetic field").[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Russell2001-94) In an approximation known as ideal [magnetohydrodynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics "Magnetohydrodynamics"), plasma only moves along magnetic field lines. As a result, the outward-flowing solar wind stretches the interplanetary magnetic field outward, forcing it into a roughly radial structure. For a simple dipolar solar magnetic field, with opposite hemispherical polarities on either side of the solar magnetic equator, a thin [current sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet "Heliospheric current sheet") is formed in the solar wind. At great distances, the rotation of the Sun twists the dipolar magnetic field and corresponding current sheet into an [Archimedean spiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_spiral "Archimedean spiral") structure called the _Parker spiral_.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Russell2001-94)

### Sunspots

[![Image 15: A black-and-white photograph of a group of sunspots.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/The_Largest_Sunspot_Group_%28noao0114%29.tiff/lossy-page1-250px-The_Largest_Sunspot_Group_%28noao0114%29.tiff.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Largest_Sunspot_Group_(noao0114).tiff)

A large sunspot group observed in white light

Sunspots are visible as dark patches on the Sun's photosphere and correspond to concentrations of magnetic field where convective transport of heat is inhibited from the solar interior to the surface. As a result, sunspots are slightly cooler than the surrounding photosphere, so they appear dark. At a typical [solar minimum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_minimum "Solar minimum"), few sunspots are visible, and occasionally none can be seen at all. Those that do appear are at high solar latitudes. As the solar cycle progresses toward its [maximum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_maximum "Solar maximum"), sunspots tend to form closer to the solar equator, a phenomenon known as _[Spörer's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sp%C3%B6rer%27s\_law "Spörer's law")_. The largest sunspots can be tens of thousands of kilometres across.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Sunspot2001-123)

An 11-year sunspot cycle is half of a 22-year [Babcock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babcock_Model "Babcock Model")–Leighton [dynamo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_dynamo "Solar dynamo") cycle, which corresponds to an oscillatory exchange of energy between [toroidal and poloidal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroidal_and_poloidal "Toroidal and poloidal") solar magnetic fields. At solar-cycle maximum, the external poloidal dipolar magnetic field is near its dynamo-cycle minimum strength; but an internal toroidal quadrupolar field, generated through differential rotation within the tachocline, is near its maximum strength. At this point in the dynamo cycle, buoyant upwelling within the convective zone forces emergence of the toroidal magnetic field through the photosphere, giving rise to pairs of sunspots, roughly aligned east–west and having footprints with opposite magnetic polarities. The magnetic polarity of sunspot pairs alternates every solar cycle, a phenomenon described by [Hale's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hale%27s_law "Hale's law").[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-124)[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-solarcycle-125)

During the solar cycle's declining phase, energy shifts from the internal toroidal magnetic field to the external poloidal field, and sunspots diminish in number and size. At solar-cycle minimum, the toroidal field is, correspondingly, at minimum strength, sunspots are relatively rare, and the poloidal field is at its maximum strength. With the rise of the next 11-year sunspot cycle, differential rotation shifts magnetic energy back from the poloidal to the toroidal field, but with a polarity that is opposite to the previous cycle. The process carries on continuously, and in an idealised, simplified scenario, each 11-year sunspot cycle corresponds to a change, then, in the overall polarity of the Sun's large-scale magnetic field.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-126)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-127)

### Solar activity

[![Image 16: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solar-cycle-data.png/250px-Solar-cycle-data.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Solar-cycle-data.png)

Measurements from 2005 of solar cycle variation during the previous 30 years

The Sun's magnetic field leads to many effects that are collectively called [solar activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_variation "Solar variation"). [Solar flares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_flares "Solar flares") and [coronal mass ejections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejections "Coronal mass ejections") tend to occur at sunspot groups. Slowly changing high-speed streams of solar wind are emitted from [coronal holes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronal_holes "Coronal holes") at the photospheric surface. Both coronal mass ejections and high-speed streams of solar wind carry plasma and the interplanetary magnetic field outward into the Solar System.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Zirker2002-128) The effects of solar activity on Earth include [auroras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy) "Aurora (astronomy)") at moderate to high latitudes and the disruption of radio communications and [electric power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_power "Electric power"). Solar activity is thought to have played a large role in the [formation and evolution of the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and evolution of the Solar System").[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-129)

Changes in solar irradiance over the 11-year solar cycle have been correlated with changes in sunspot number.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-130) The solar cycle influences [space weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_weather "Space weather") conditions, including those surrounding Earth. For example, in the 17th century, the solar cycle appeared to have stopped entirely for several decades; few sunspots were observed during a period known as the [Maunder minimum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maunder_minimum "Maunder minimum"). This coincided in time with the era of the [Little Ice Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Little_Ice_Age "Little Ice Age"), when Europe experienced unusually cold temperatures.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-131)[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Lean-132) Earlier extended minima have been discovered through analysis of [tree rings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_ring "Tree ring") and appear to have coincided with lower-than-average global temperatures.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-133)

### Coronal heating

Unsolved problem in astronomy

Why is the Sun's corona so much hotter than the Sun's surface?

The temperature of the photosphere is approximately 6,000 K, whereas the temperature of the corona reaches 1,000,000–2,000,000 K.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91) The high temperature of the corona shows that it is heated by something other than direct [heat conduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_conduction "Heat conduction") from the photosphere.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Russell2001-94)

It is thought that the energy necessary to heat the corona is provided by turbulent motion in the convection zone below the photosphere, and two main mechanisms have been proposed to explain coronal heating.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91) The first is wave heating, in which sound, gravitational or magnetohydrodynamic waves are produced by turbulence in the convection zone.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91) These waves travel upward and dissipate in the corona, depositing their energy in the ambient matter in the form of heat.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Alfven-134) The other is magnetic heating, in which magnetic energy is continuously built up by photospheric motion and released through [magnetic reconnection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection "Magnetic reconnection") in the form of large solar flares and myriad similar but smaller events—[nanoflares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoflares "Nanoflares").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Parker2-135)

Currently, it is unclear whether waves are an efficient heating mechanism. All waves except Alfvén waves have been found to dissipate or refract before reaching the corona.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Sturrock-136) In addition, Alfvén waves do not easily dissipate in the corona. The current research focus has therefore shifted toward flare heating mechanisms.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-91)

Life phases
-----------

[![Image 17: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/The_life_cycle_of_a_Sun-like_star_%28annotated%29.jpg/500px-The_life_cycle_of_a_Sun-like_star_%28annotated%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_life_cycle_of_a_Sun-like_star_(annotated).jpg)

Overview of the evolution of a star like the Sun, from collapsing [protostar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protostar "Protostar") at left to [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") stage at right

The Sun today is roughly halfway through the main-sequence portion of its life. It has not changed dramatically in over four billion[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-short-17) years and will remain fairly stable for about five billion more. However, after hydrogen fusion in its core has stopped, the Sun will undergo dramatic changes, both internally and externally.

### Formation

The Sun formed about 4.6 billion years ago from the collapse of part of a giant [molecular cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_cloud "Molecular cloud") that consisted mostly of hydrogen and helium and that probably gave birth to many other stars.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Zirker2002-7-137) This age is estimated using [computer models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_simulation "Computer simulation") of [stellar evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_evolution "Stellar evolution") and through [nucleocosmochronology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology "Nucleocosmochronology").[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Bonanno-13) The result is consistent with the [radiometric date](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiometric_dating "Radiometric dating") of the oldest Solar System material, at 4.567 billion years ago.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-138)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-nature436-139) Studies of ancient [meteorites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorite "Meteorite") reveal traces of stable daughter nuclei of short-lived isotopes, such as [iron-60](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron-60 "Iron-60"), that form only in exploding, short-lived stars. This indicates that one or more [supernovae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova "Supernova") must have occurred near the location where the Sun formed. A [shock wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shock_wave "Shock wave") from a nearby supernova would have triggered the formation of the Sun by compressing the matter within the molecular cloud and causing certain regions to collapse under their own gravity.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-140) As one fragment of the cloud collapsed it also began to rotate due to [conservation of angular momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum "Conservation of angular momentum") and heat up with the increasing pressure.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-141) Much of the mass became concentrated in the centre, whereas the rest flattened out into a disk that would become the planets and other Solar System bodies.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-142)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-143) Gravity and pressure within the core of the cloud generated a lot of heat as it accumulated more matter from the surrounding disk, eventually triggering [nuclear fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis "Stellar nucleosynthesis").[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-144)

The stars [HD 162826](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HD_162826 "HD 162826") and [HD 186302](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HD_186302 "HD 186302") share similarities with the Sun and are hypothesised to be its stellar siblings, formed in the same molecular cloud.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-145)[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-146)

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/The_violent_youth_of_solar_proxies.jpg/250px-The_violent_youth_of_solar_proxies.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_violent_youth_of_solar_proxies.jpg)

The violent youth of stars like the Sun

### Main sequence

[![Image 19: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Evolution_of_the_Sun_2_EN.svg/500px-Evolution_of_the_Sun_2_EN.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Evolution_of_the_Sun_2_EN.svg)

Evolution of a Sun-like star. The track of a one solar mass star on the [Hertzsprung–Russell diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram "Hertzsprung–Russell diagram") is shown from the main sequence to the white dwarf stage.

The Sun is about halfway through its main-sequence stage, during which nuclear fusion reactions in its core fuse hydrogen into helium. Each second, more than four billion kilograms of matter are converted into energy within the Sun's core, producing neutrinos and [solar radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radiation "Solar radiation"). At this rate, the Sun has so far converted around 100 times the mass of Earth into energy, about 0.03% of the total mass of the Sun. The Sun will spend a total of approximately 10 to 11 billion years as a main-sequence star before the [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") phase of the Sun.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-147) At the 8 billion year mark, the Sun will be at its hottest point according to the ESA's _[Gaia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia\_(spacecraft) "Gaia (spacecraft)")_ space observatory mission in 2022.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-148)

The Sun is gradually becoming hotter in its core, hotter at the surface, larger in radius, and more luminous during its time on the main sequence: since the beginning of its main sequence life, it has expanded in radius by 15% and the surface has increased in temperature from 5,620 K (9,660°F) to 5,772 K (9,930°F), resulting in a 48% increase in luminosity from 0.677 [solar luminosities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_luminosity "Solar luminosity") to its present-day 1.0 solar luminosity. This occurs because the helium atoms in the core have a higher mean [molecular weight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_weight "Molecular weight") than the [hydrogen atoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_atom "Hydrogen atom") that were fused, resulting in less thermal pressure. The core is therefore shrinking, allowing the outer layers of the Sun to move closer to the centre, releasing [gravitational potential energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy "Gravitational potential energy"). According to the [virial theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virial_theorem "Virial theorem"), half of this released gravitational energy goes into heating, which leads to a gradual increase in the rate at which fusion occurs and thus an increase in the luminosity. This process speeds up as the core gradually becomes denser.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-carroll_ostlie-149) At present, it is increasing in brightness by about 1% every 100 million years. It will take at least 1 billion years from now to deplete liquid water from the Earth from such increase.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-150) After that, the Earth will cease to be able to support complex, multicellular life and the last remaining multicellular organisms on the planet will suffer a final, complete [mass extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_extinction "Mass extinction").[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Snyder-BeattieAndBonsall2022-151)

### After core hydrogen exhaustion

[![Image 20: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Sun_red_giant.svg/250px-Sun_red_giant.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sun_red_giant.svg)

The size of the current Sun (now in the [main sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Main_sequence "Main sequence")) compared to its estimated size during its red-giant phase in the future

The Sun does not have enough mass to explode as a [supernova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova "Supernova"). Instead, when it runs out of hydrogen in the core in approximately 5 billion years, core hydrogen fusion will stop, and there will be nothing to prevent the core from contracting. The release of gravitational potential energy will cause the luminosity of the Sun to increase, ending the main sequence phase and leading the Sun to expand over the next billion years: first into a [subgiant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subgiant "Subgiant"), and then into a [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant").[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-carroll_ostlie-149)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-152)[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153) The heating due to gravitational contraction will also lead to expansion of the Sun and hydrogen fusion in a shell just outside the core, where unfused hydrogen remains, contributing to the increased luminosity, which will eventually reach more than 1,000 times its present luminosity.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-carroll_ostlie-149) When the Sun enters its [red-giant branch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-giant_branch "Red-giant branch") (RGB) phase, it will engulf (and destroy) [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)") and [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus "Venus"). According to a 2008 article, Earth's orbit will have initially expanded to at most 1.5 AU (220 million km; 140 million mi) due to the Sun's loss of mass. However, Earth's orbit will then start shrinking due to [tidal forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_forces "Tidal forces") (and, eventually, drag from the lower chromosphere) so that it is engulfed by the Sun during the [tip of the red-giant branch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tip_of_the_red-giant_branch "Tip of the red-giant branch") phase 7.59 billion years from now, 3.8 and 1 million years after Mercury and Venus have respectively suffered the same fate.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153)

By the time the Sun reaches the tip of the red-giant branch, it will be about 256 times larger than it is today, with a radius of 1.19 AU (178 million km; 111 million mi).[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153)[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-sackmann-154) The Sun will spend around a billion years in the RGB and lose around a third of its mass.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153)

After the red-giant branch, the Sun has approximately 120 million years of active life left, but much happens. First, the core (full of [degenerate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degenerate_matter "Degenerate matter") helium) ignites violently in the [helium flash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium_flash "Helium flash"); it is estimated that 6% of the core—itself 40% of the Sun's mass—will be converted into carbon within a matter of minutes through the [triple-alpha process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triple-alpha_process "Triple-alpha process").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-155) The Sun then shrinks to around 10 times its current size and 50 times the luminosity, with a temperature a little lower than today. It will then have reached the [red clump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_clump "Red clump") or [horizontal branch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizontal_branch "Horizontal branch"), but a star of the Sun's metallicity does not evolve blueward along the horizontal branch. Instead, it just becomes moderately larger and more luminous over about 100 million years as it continues to react helium in the core.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153)

When the helium is exhausted, the Sun will repeat the expansion it followed when the hydrogen in the core was exhausted. This time, however, it all happens faster, and the Sun becomes larger and more luminous. This is the [asymptotic-giant-branch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch "Asymptotic giant branch") phase, and the Sun is alternately reacting hydrogen in a shell or helium in a deeper shell. After about 20 million years on the early asymptotic giant branch, the Sun becomes increasingly unstable, with rapid mass loss and [thermal pulses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_pulse "Thermal pulse") that increase the size and luminosity for a few hundred years every 100,000 years or so. The thermal pulses become larger each time, with the later pulses pushing the luminosity to as much as 5,000 times the current level. Despite this, the Sun's maximum AGB radius will not be as large as its tip-RGB maximum: 179 [R☉](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radius "Solar radius"), or about 0.832 AU (124.5 million km; 77.3 million mi).[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-agb-156)

Models vary depending on the rate and timing of mass loss. Models that have higher mass loss on the red-giant branch produce smaller, less luminous stars at the tip of the asymptotic giant branch, perhaps only 2,000 times the luminosity and less than 200 times the radius.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153) For the Sun, four thermal pulses are predicted before it completely loses its outer envelope and starts to make a [planetary nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_nebula "Planetary nebula").[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-SunIII-157)

The post-asymptotic-giant-branch evolution is even faster. The luminosity stays approximately constant as the temperature increases, with the ejected half of the Sun's mass becoming ionised into a [planetary nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_nebula "Planetary nebula") as the exposed core reaches 30,000 K (53,500°F), as if it is in a sort of [blue loop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_loop "Blue loop"). The final naked core, a [white dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_dwarf "White dwarf"), will have a temperature of over 100,000 K (180,000°F) and contain an estimated 54.05% of the Sun's present-day mass.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-schroder-153) Simulations indicate that the Sun may be among the least massive stars capable of forming a planetary nebula.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-158) The planetary nebula will disperse in about 10,000 years, but the white dwarf will survive for trillions of years before fading to a hypothetical super-dense [black dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_dwarf "Black dwarf").[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-bloecker1-159)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-bloecker2-160)[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-161) As such, it would give off no more energy.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Johnson-Groh_2020_j255-162)

Location
--------

### Solar System

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/250px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg)

Location of the Sun within the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), which extends to the edge of the [Oort cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oort_cloud "Oort cloud"), where at 125,000 AU to 230,000 AU, equal to several light-years, the Sun's [gravitational sphere of influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astrodynamics) "Sphere of influence (astrodynamics)") ends.

The Sun has eight known planets orbiting it. This includes four [terrestrial planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planets "Terrestrial planets") ([Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)"), [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus "Venus"), [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth"), and [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars")), two [gas giants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_giants "Gas giants") ([Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") and [Saturn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturn "Saturn")), and two [ice giants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_giants "Ice giants") ([Uranus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus "Uranus") and [Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune "Neptune")).
