The Solar System also has nine bodies generally considered as [dwarf planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet "Dwarf planet") and some more [candidates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets "List of possible dwarf planets"), an [asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt "Asteroid belt"), numerous [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comets "Comets"), and a large number of icy bodies which lie beyond the orbit of Neptune. Six of the planets and many smaller bodies also have their own [natural satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite "Natural satellite"): in particular, the satellite systems of Jupiter, Saturn, and Uranus are in some ways like miniature versions of the Sun's system.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-163)

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Solar_system_barycenter.svg/250px-Solar_system_barycenter.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Solar_system_barycenter.svg)

Apparent motion of the Solar System [barycentre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barycentre "Barycentre") with respect to the Sun

The Sun is moved by the gravitational pull of the planets. The centre of the Sun moves around the Solar System [barycentre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barycentre "Barycentre"), within a range from 0.1 to 2.2 solar radii. The Sun's motion around the barycentre approximately repeats every 179 years, rotated by about 30° due primarily to the [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period "Synodic period") of Jupiter and Saturn.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-164) This motion is mainly due to Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune. For some periods of several decades (when Neptune and Uranus are in [opposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy) "Opposition (astronomy)")) the motion is rather regular, forming a [trefoil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trefoil "Trefoil") pattern, whereas between these periods it appears more chaotic.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-165) After 179 years (nine times the [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period "Synodic period") of Jupiter and Saturn), the pattern more or less repeats, but rotated by about 24°.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-166) The orbits of the inner planets, including of the Earth, are similarly displaced by the same gravitational forces, so the movement of the Sun has little effect on the relative positions of the Earth and the Sun or on solar irradiance on the Earth as a function of time.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-167)

The Sun's gravitational field is estimated to [dominate the gravitational forces of surrounding stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astrodynamics) "Sphere of influence (astrodynamics)") out to about two light-years (125,000 AU). Lower estimates for the radius of the [Oort cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oort_cloud "Oort cloud"), by contrast, do not place it farther than 50,000 AU.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Encrenaz_et_al_2004-168) Most of the mass is orbiting in the region between 3,000 and 100,000 AU.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-169) The furthest known objects, such as [Comet West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet_West "Comet West"), have aphelia around 70,000 AU from the Sun.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-170) The Sun's [Hill sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hill_sphere "Hill sphere") with respect to the galactic nucleus, the effective range of its gravitational influence, was calculated by [G. A. Chebotarev](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gleb_Alexandrovich_Chebotarev&action=edit&redlink=1 "Gleb Alexandrovich Chebotarev (page does not exist)") to be 230,000 AU.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Chebotarev-171)

### Celestial neighbourhood

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/The_Local_Interstellar_Cloud_and_neighboring_G-cloud_complex.svg/250px-The_Local_Interstellar_Cloud_and_neighboring_G-cloud_complex.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Local_Interstellar_Cloud_and_neighboring_G-cloud_complex.svg)

Diagram of the [Local Interstellar Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud "Local Interstellar Cloud"), the [G-Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-Cloud "G-Cloud") and surrounding stars. As of 2022, the exact position of the Solar System within the interstellar clouds remains an unresolved question in astronomy.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-172)

Within 10 light-years of the Sun there are relatively few stars, the closest being the triple star system [Alpha Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Centauri "Alpha Centauri"), which is about 4.4 light-years away and may be in the Local Bubble's [G-Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-Cloud "G-Cloud").[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-173) Alpha Centauri A and B are a closely tied pair of [Sun-like stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_analog "Solar analog"), whereas the closest star to the Sun, the small [red dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf")[Proxima Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxima_Centauri "Proxima Centauri"), orbits the pair at a distance of 0.2 light-years. In 2016, a potentially habitable [exoplanet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet "Exoplanet") was found to be orbiting Proxima Centauri, called [Proxima Centauri b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b "Proxima Centauri b"), the closest confirmed exoplanet to the Sun.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_System_proxima_b_discovery_paper-174)

The Solar System is surrounded by the [Local Interstellar Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud "Local Interstellar Cloud"), although it is not clear if it is embedded in the Local Interstellar Cloud or if it lies just outside the cloud's edge.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_System_:1-175) Multiple other [interstellar clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_cloud "Interstellar cloud") exist in the region within 300 light-years of the Sun, known as the [Local Bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Bubble "Local Bubble").[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_System_:1-175) The latter feature is an hourglass-shaped cavity or [superbubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superbubble "Superbubble") in the interstellar medium roughly 300 light-years across. The bubble is suffused with high-temperature plasma, suggesting that it may be the product of several recent supernovae.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-176)

The Local Bubble is a small superbubble compared to the neighboring wider [Radcliffe Wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radcliffe_Wave "Radcliffe Wave") and _Split_ linear structures (formerly [Gould Belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gould_Belt "Gould Belt")), each of which are some thousands of light-years in length.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_System_Alves_Zucker_Goodman_Speagle_2020-177) All these structures are part of the [Orion Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Arm "Orion Arm"), which contains most of the stars in the Milky Way that are visible to the unaided eye.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-178)

Groups of stars form together in [star clusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_cluster "Star cluster"), before dissolving into co-moving associations. A prominent grouping that is visible to the naked eye is the [Ursa Major moving group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ursa_Major_moving_group "Ursa Major moving group"), which is around 80 light-years away within the Local Bubble. The nearest star cluster is [Hyades](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyades_(star_cluster) "Hyades (star cluster)"), which lies at the edge of the Local Bubble. The closest star-forming regions are the [Corona Australis Molecular Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corona_Australis_Molecular_Cloud "Corona Australis Molecular Cloud"), the [Rho Ophiuchi cloud complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rho_Ophiuchi_cloud_complex "Rho Ophiuchi cloud complex") and the [Taurus molecular cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taurus_molecular_cloud "Taurus molecular cloud"); the latter lies just beyond the Local Bubble and is part of the Radcliffe wave.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-179)

Stellar flybys that pass within 0.8 light-years of the Sun occur roughly once every 100,000 years. The [closest well-measured approach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars#Distant_future_and_past_encounters "List of nearest stars") was [Scholz's Star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scholz%27s_Star "Scholz's Star"), which approached to ~50,000 AU of the Sun some ~70 thousands years ago, likely passing through the outer Oort cloud.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-180) There is a 1% chance every billion years that a star will pass within 100 AU of the Sun, potentially disrupting the Solar System.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Solar_System_Raymond_et_al_2024-181)

Motion
------

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Motion_of_Sun%2C_Earth_and_Moon_around_the_Milky_Way.jpg/250px-Motion_of_Sun%2C_Earth_and_Moon_around_the_Milky_Way.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Motion_of_Sun,_Earth_and_Moon_around_the_Milky_Way.jpg)

The general motion and orientation of the Sun, with Earth and the Moon as its Solar System satellites

The Sun, taking along the whole Solar System, orbits [the galaxy's centre of mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center") at an average speed of 230 km/s (828,000 km/h),[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-StarChild-182) taking about 220–250 million [Earth years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_year "Earth year") to complete a revolution (a [galactic year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_year "Galactic year")), having done so about 20 times since the Sun's formation.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Currin_2020_t043-183)[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-fn10-184) The direction of the Sun's motion, the [Solar apex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_apex "Solar apex"), is roughly in the direction of the star [Vega](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vega "Vega").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-185) In the past the Sun likely moved trough the [Orion–Eridanus Superbubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion%E2%80%93Eridanus_Superbubble "Orion–Eridanus Superbubble"), before entering the Local Bubble.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-:2-186)

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/250px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg)

The Sun's idealised orbit around the Galactic Centre in an artist's top-down depiction of the current layout of the Milky Way

As the sun goes around the galaxy it also moves with respect to the average motion of the other stars around it. A simple model predicts that in a frame of reference rotating with the galaxy, the sun moves in an ellipse, circulating around a point that is itself going around the galaxy.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-187) The period of the Sun's circulation around the point is about 166 million years, shorter than the time it takes for the point to go around the galaxy. The length of the ellipse is around 1760[parsecs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec "Parsec") and its width around 1170 parsecs. (Compare this to the distance of the Sun from the centre of the galaxy, around 7 or 8 kiloparsecs.) At the same time, the sun moves "north" and "south" of the galactic plane with a different period, around 83 million years, moving about 99 parsecs away from the plane.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-188) The point around which the Sun circulates takes around 240 million years to go once around the galaxy. (See [Stellar kinematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_kinematics "Stellar kinematics") for more details.)

The Sun's orbit around the Milky Way is perturbed due to the non-uniform mass distribution in Milky Way, such as that in and between the galactic spiral arms. It has been argued that the Sun's passage through the higher density spiral arms often coincides with [mass extinctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_extinction "Mass extinction") on Earth, perhaps due to increased [impact events](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_events "Impact events").[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-extinction-189) It takes the Solar System about 225–250 million years to complete one orbit through the Milky Way (a _[galactic year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic\_year "Galactic year")_),[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-fn10-184) so it is thought to have completed 20–25 orbits during the lifetime of the Sun. The [orbital speed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_speed "Orbital speed") of the Solar System about the centre of the Milky Way is approximately 251 km/s (156 mi/s).[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-space.newscientist.com-190) At this speed, it takes around 1,190 years for the Solar System to travel a distance of 1 light-year, or 7 days to travel 1 AU.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-191)

The Milky Way is moving with respect to the [cosmic microwave background radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation "Cosmic microwave background radiation") (CMB) in the direction of the constellation [Hydra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydra_(constellation) "Hydra (constellation)") with a speed of 550 km/s, but since the Sun is moving with respect to the Galactic Centre in the direction of Cygnus (galactic longitude 90°; latitude 0°) at more than 200 km/sec, the resultant velocity with respect to the CMB is about 370 km/s in the direction of [Crater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crater_(constellation) "Crater (constellation)") or [Leo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leo_(constellation) "Leo (constellation)") (galactic latitude 264°, latitude 48°).[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-192) This is 132° away from Cygnus.

Observational history
---------------------

### Early understanding

[![Image 26: A sculpture of the sun in a chariot being pulled by a horse that has wheels instead of hoofs.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg/250px-Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg)

The [Trundholm sun chariot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trundholm_sun_chariot "Trundholm sun chariot") pulled by a horse is a sculpture believed to be illustrating an important part of [Nordic Bronze Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age "Nordic Bronze Age") mythology.

In many prehistoric and ancient cultures, the Sun was thought to be a [solar deity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_deity "Solar deity") or other [supernatural](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernatural "Supernatural") entity.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-e488-193)[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-t793-194) In the early 1st millennium BC, [Babylonian astronomers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy "Babylonian astronomy") observed that the Sun's motion along the [ecliptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic "Ecliptic") is not uniform, though they did not know why; it is today known that this is due to the movement of Earth in an [elliptic orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_orbit "Elliptic orbit"), moving faster when it is nearer to the Sun at perihelion and moving slower when it is farther away at aphelion.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-195)

One of the first people to offer a scientific or philosophical explanation for the Sun was the [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece") philosopher [Anaxagoras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaxagoras "Anaxagoras"). He reasoned that it was a giant flaming ball of metal even larger than the land of the [Peloponnesus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peloponnese "Peloponnese") and that the Moon reflected the light of the Sun.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-196)[Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eratosthenes "Eratosthenes") estimated the distance between Earth and the Sun in the 3rd century BC as "of stadia [myriads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myriad "Myriad") 400 and 80000", the translation of which is ambiguous, implying either 4,080,000 [stadia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stadion_(unit) "Stadion (unit)") (755,000 km) or 804,000,000 stadia (148 to 153 million kilometres or 0.99 to 1.02 AU); the latter value is correct to within a few per cent. In the 1st century AD, [Ptolemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptolemy "Ptolemy") estimated the distance as 1,210 times [the radius of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_radius "Earth radius"), approximately 7.71 million kilometres (0.0515 AU).[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-197)

The theory that the Sun is the centre around which the planets orbit was first proposed by the ancient Greek [Aristarchus of Samos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos "Aristarchus of Samos") in the 3rd century BC,[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-198) and later adopted by [Seleucus of Seleucia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia "Seleucus of Seleucia") (see [Heliocentrism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliocentrism "Heliocentrism")).[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-199) This view was developed in a more detailed mathematical model of a heliocentric system in the 16th century by [Nicolaus Copernicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus "Nicolaus Copernicus").[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-200)

### Development of scientific understanding

[![Image 27: A drawing of a man wearing a crown in a chariot, being pulled by horses.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sun-bonatti.png/250px-Sun-bonatti.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sun-bonatti.png)

Sol, the Personification of the Sun, from a 1550 edition of [Guido Bonatti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guido_Bonatti "Guido Bonatti")'s _\_Liber astronomiae\__

Observations of sunspots were recorded by [Chinese astronomers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_astronomers "Chinese astronomers") during the [Han dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Han_dynasty "Han dynasty") (202 BC– AD 220), with records of their observations being maintained for centuries. [Averroes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Averroes "Averroes") also provided a description of sunspots in the 12th century.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-201) The invention of the telescope in the early 17th century permitted detailed observations of sunspots by [Thomas Harriot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Harriot "Thomas Harriot"), [Galileo Galilei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") and other astronomers. Galileo posited that sunspots were on the surface of the Sun rather than small objects passing between Earth and the Sun.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-202)

[Medieval Islamic astronomical contributions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world "Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world") include [al-Battani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Battani "Al-Battani")'s discovery that the direction of the Sun's [apogee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apogee "Apogee") (the place in the Sun's orbit against the fixed stars where it seems to be moving slowest) is changing.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-203) In modern heliocentric terms, this is caused by a gradual motion of the aphelion of the _Earth's_ orbit. [Ibn Yunus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Yunus "Ibn Yunus") observed more than 10,000 entries for the Sun's position for many years using a large [astrolabe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrolabe "Astrolabe").[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-204)

The first reasonably accurate distance to the Sun was determined in 1684 by [Giovanni Domenico Cassini](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini "Giovanni Domenico Cassini"). Knowing that direct measurements of the solar parallax were difficult, he chose to measure the Martian parallax. Having sent [Jean Richer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Richer "Jean Richer") to [Cayenne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cayenne "Cayenne"), part of [French Guiana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Guiana "French Guiana"), for simultaneous measurements, Cassini in Paris determined the parallax of [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars") when Mars was at its closest to Earth in 1672. Using the circumference distance between the two observations, Cassini calculated the Earth-Mars distance, then used [Kepler's laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws "Kepler's laws") to determine the Earth-Sun distance. His value, about 10% smaller than modern values, was much larger than all previous estimates.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-205)

From an observation of a [transit of Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transit_of_Venus "Transit of Venus") in 1032, the Persian astronomer and polymath [Ibn Sina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avicenna "Avicenna") concluded that Venus was closer to Earth than the Sun.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Goldstein-206) In 1677, [Edmond Halley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmond_Halley "Edmond Halley") observed a transit of Mercury across the Sun, leading him to realise that observations of the [solar parallax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_parallax "Solar parallax") of a planet (more ideally using the transit of Venus) could be used to [trigonometrically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometry "Trigonometry") determine the distances between Earth, [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus "Venus"), and the Sun.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-207) Careful observations of the [1769 transit of Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1769_transit_of_Venus_observed_from_Tahiti "1769 transit of Venus observed from Tahiti") allowed astronomers to calculate the average Earth–Sun distance as 93,726,900 miles (150,838,800 km), only 0.8% greater than the modern value.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-208)

[![Image 28: A photograph of the sun](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/BBSO_full-disk_H-alpha_2002-07-26_153931_color.png/250px-BBSO_full-disk_H-alpha_2002-07-26_153931_color.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BBSO_full-disk_H-alpha_2002-07-26_153931_color.png)

Sun as seen in Hydrogen-alpha light

In 1666, [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") observed the Sun's light using a [prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_(optics) "Prism (optics)"), and showed that it is made up of light of many colours.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-209) In 1800, [William Herschel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Herschel "William Herschel") discovered [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") radiation beyond the red part of the solar spectrum.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-210) The 19th century saw advancement in spectroscopic studies of the Sun; [Joseph von Fraunhofer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_von_Fraunhofer "Joseph von Fraunhofer") recorded more than 600 [absorption lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_lines "Absorption lines") in the spectrum, the strongest of which are still often referred to as _[Fraunhofer lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fraunhofer\_lines "Fraunhofer lines")_. The 20th century brought about several specialised systems for observing the Sun, especially at different narrowband wavelengths, such as those using Calcium-H (396.9 nm), Calcium-K (393.37 nm) and [Hydrogen-alpha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen-alpha "Hydrogen-alpha") (656.46 nm) [filtering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_filter "Astronomical filter").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-211)

During early studies of the [optical spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_spectrum "Optical spectrum") of the photosphere, some absorption lines were found that did not correspond to any [chemical elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical element") then known on Earth. In 1868, [Norman Lockyer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_Lockyer "Norman Lockyer") hypothesised that these absorption lines were caused by a new element that he dubbed _helium_, after the Greek Sun god [Helios](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helios "Helios"). Twenty-five years later, helium was isolated on Earth.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Lockyer-212)

In the early years of the modern scientific era, the source of the Sun's energy was a significant puzzle. [Lord Kelvin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Kelvin "Lord Kelvin") suggested that the Sun is a gradually cooling liquid body that is radiating an internal store of heat.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-kelvin-213) Kelvin and [Hermann von Helmholtz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz "Hermann von Helmholtz") then proposed a [gravitational contraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism "Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism") mechanism to explain the energy output, but the resulting age estimate was only 20 million years, well short of the time span of at least 300 million years suggested by some geological discoveries of that time.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-kelvin-213)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-214) In 1890, Lockyer proposed a meteoritic hypothesis for the formation and evolution of the Sun.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-215)

Not until 1904 was a documented solution offered. [Ernest Rutherford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford "Ernest Rutherford") suggested that the Sun's output could be maintained by an internal source of heat, and suggested [radioactive decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_decay "Radioactive decay") as the source.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-216) However, it would be [Albert Einstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein "Albert Einstein") who would provide the essential clue to the source of the Sun's energy output with his [mass–energy equivalence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence "Mass–energy equivalence") relation _E_ = _mc_ 2.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-217) In 1920, Sir [Arthur Eddington](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Eddington "Arthur Eddington") proposed that the pressures and temperatures at the core of the Sun could produce a nuclear fusion reaction that merged hydrogen (protons) into helium nuclei, resulting in a production of energy from the net change in mass.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-218) The preponderance of hydrogen in the Sun was confirmed in 1925 by [Cecilia Payne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cecilia_Payne-Gaposchkin "Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin") using the ionisation theory developed by [Meghnad Saha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meghnad_Saha "Meghnad Saha"). The theoretical concept of fusion was developed in the 1930s by the astrophysicists [Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar "Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar") and [Hans Bethe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Bethe "Hans Bethe"). Bethe calculated the details of the two main energy-producing nuclear reactions that power the Sun.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Bethe-219)[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Bethe2-220) In 1957, [Margaret Burbidge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Burbidge "Margaret Burbidge"), [Geoffrey Burbidge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Burbidge "Geoffrey Burbidge"), [William Fowler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Alfred_Fowler "William Alfred Fowler") and [Fred Hoyle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fred_Hoyle "Fred Hoyle") showed that most of the elements in the universe have been [synthesised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleosynthesis "Nucleosynthesis") by nuclear reactions inside stars, some like the Sun.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-221)

### Solar space missions

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Pioneer-6-9.jpg/250px-Pioneer-6-9.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pioneer-6-9.jpg)

[_Pioneer 6_, _7_, _8_, and _9_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pioneer_6,_7,_8,_and_9 "Pioneer 6, 7, 8, and 9")

The first satellites designed for long term observation of the Sun from interplanetary space were [_Pioneer 6_, _7_, _8_, and _9_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pioneer_6,_7,_8,_and_9 "Pioneer 6, 7, 8, and 9"), which were launched by NASA between 1959 and 1968. These probes orbited the Sun at a distance similar to that of Earth, and made the first detailed measurements of the solar wind and the solar magnetic field. _Pioneer 9_ operated for a particularly long time, transmitting data until May 1983.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-222)[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-223)

In the 1970s, two [_Helios_ spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helios_(spacecraft) "Helios (spacecraft)") and the Skylab [Apollo Telescope Mount](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount "Apollo Telescope Mount") provided scientists with significant new data on solar wind and the solar corona. The _Helios 1_ and _2_ probes were U.S.–German collaborations that studied the solar wind from an orbit carrying the spacecraft inside Mercury's orbit at perihelion.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Burlaga2001-224) The Skylab space station, launched by NASA in 1973, included a solar observatory module called the Apollo Telescope Mount that was operated by astronauts resident on the station.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-92) Skylab made the first time-resolved observations of the solar transition region and of ultraviolet emissions from the solar corona.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-92) Discoveries included the first observations of coronal mass ejections, then called "coronal transients", and of [coronal holes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronal_hole "Coronal hole"), now known to be intimately associated with the solar wind.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Burlaga2001-224)

[![Image 30: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Smm.jpg/250px-Smm.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Smm.jpg)

Drawing of a [Solar Maximum Mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_Maximum_Mission "Solar Maximum Mission") probe

In 1980, the [Solar Maximum Mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_Maximum_Mission "Solar Maximum Mission") probes were launched by NASA. This spacecraft was designed to observe gamma rays, [X-rays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray "X-ray") and [ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet") radiation from solar flares during a time of high solar activity and solar luminosity. Just a few months after launch, however, an electronics failure caused the probe to go into standby mode, and it spent the next three years in this inactive state. In 1984, [Space Shuttle _Challenger_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger "Space Shuttle Challenger") mission [STS-41-C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-41-C "STS-41-C") retrieved the satellite and repaired its electronics before re-releasing it into orbit. The Solar Maximum Mission subsequently acquired thousands of images of the solar corona before [re-entering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry "Atmospheric reentry") Earth's atmosphere in June 1989.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-225)

Launched in 1991, Japan's [Yohkoh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yohkoh "Yohkoh") (_Sunbeam_) satellite observed solar flares at X-ray wavelengths. Mission data allowed scientists to identify several different types of flares and demonstrated that the corona away from regions of peak activity was much more dynamic and active than had previously been supposed. Yohkoh observed an entire solar cycle but went into standby mode when an annular eclipse in 2001 caused it to lose its lock on the Sun. It was destroyed by atmospheric re-entry in 2005.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-226)

The [Solar and Heliospheric Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory "Solar and Heliospheric Observatory"), jointly built by the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") and NASA, was launched on 2 December 1995.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-92) Originally intended to serve a two-year mission,[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-227) SOHO remains in operation as of 2024.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-228) Situated at the [Lagrangian point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_point "Lagrangian point") between Earth and the Sun (at which the gravitational pull from both is equal), SOHO has provided a constant view of the Sun at many wavelengths since its launch.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-92) Besides its direct solar observation, SOHO has enabled the discovery of a large number of [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet"), mostly tiny [sungrazing comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sungrazing_comet "Sungrazing comet") that incinerate as they pass the Sun.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-229)

[![Image 31: A photograph of Ulysses spacecraft](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/The_Ulysses_spacecraft_undergoes_testing_at_the_vacuum_spin-balancing_facility_in_ESTEC.jpg/250px-The_Ulysses_spacecraft_undergoes_testing_at_the_vacuum_spin-balancing_facility_in_ESTEC.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Ulysses_spacecraft_undergoes_testing_at_the_vacuum_spin-balancing_facility_in_ESTEC.jpg)

[_Ulysses_ spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft) "Ulysses (spacecraft)") testing at the vacuum spin-balancing facility

All these satellites have observed the Sun from the plane of the ecliptic, and so have only observed its equatorial regions in detail. The [_Ulysses_ probe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft) "Ulysses (spacecraft)") was launched in 1990 to study the Sun's polar regions. It first travelled to Jupiter, to "slingshot" into an orbit that would take it far above the plane of the ecliptic. Once _Ulysses_ was in its scheduled orbit, it began observing the solar wind and magnetic field strength at high solar latitudes, finding that the solar wind from high latitudes was moving at about 750 km/s, which was slower than expected, and that there were large magnetic waves emerging from high latitudes that scattered galactic cosmic rays.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-230)

Elemental abundances in the photosphere are well known from [spectroscopic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy "Astronomical spectroscopy") studies, but the composition of the interior of the Sun is more poorly understood. A solar wind sample return mission, _[Genesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genesis\_(spacecraft) "Genesis (spacecraft)")_, was designed to allow astronomers to directly measure the composition of solar material.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-231)

Observation by eyes
-------------------

### Exposure to the eye

[![Image 32: See caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/-_panoramio_%28785%29.jpg/250px--_panoramio_%28785%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:-_panoramio_(785).jpg)

The Sun seen from Earth, with [glare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glare_(vision) "Glare (vision)") from the lenses. The eye also sees glare when looked towards the Sun directly.

The brightness of the Sun can cause pain from looking at it with the [naked eye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naked_eye "Naked eye"); however, doing so for brief periods is not hazardous for normal non-[dilated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mydriasis "Mydriasis") eyes.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-232)[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-233) Looking directly at the Sun, known as [sungazing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sungazing "Sungazing"), causes [phosphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phosphene "Phosphene") visual artefacts and temporary partial blindness. It also delivers about 4 milliwatts of sunlight to the retina, slightly heating it and potentially causing damage in eyes that cannot respond properly to the brightness.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-234)[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-235) Viewing of the direct Sun with the naked eye can cause UV-induced, sunburn-like lesions on the retina beginning after about 100 seconds, particularly under conditions where the UV light from the Sun is intense and well focused.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-236)[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-237)

Viewing the Sun through light-concentrating [optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optics "Optics") such as [binoculars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binoculars "Binoculars") may result in permanent damage to the retina without an appropriate filter that blocks UV and substantially dims the sunlight. When using an attenuating filter to view the Sun, the viewer is cautioned to use a filter specifically designed for that use. Some improvised filters that pass UV or [IR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") rays, can actually harm the eye at high brightness levels.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-238) Brief glances at the midday Sun through an unfiltered telescope can cause permanent damage.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Macdonald-239)

During sunrise and sunset, sunlight is attenuated because of [Rayleigh scattering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering "Rayleigh scattering") and [Mie scattering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mie_theory "Mie theory") from a particularly long passage through Earth's atmosphere,[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Haber2005-240) and the Sun is sometimes faint enough to be viewed comfortably with the naked eye or safely with optics (provided there is no risk of bright sunlight suddenly appearing through a break between clouds). Hazy conditions, atmospheric dust, and high humidity contribute to this atmospheric attenuation.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-241)

### Phenomena

An [optical phenomenon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_phenomenon "Optical phenomenon"), known as a [green flash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_flash "Green flash"), can sometimes be seen shortly after sunset or before sunrise. The flash is caused by light from the Sun just below the horizon being [bent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refraction "Refraction") (usually through a [temperature inversion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperature_inversion "Temperature inversion")) towards the observer. Light of shorter wavelengths (violet, blue, green) is bent more than that of longer wavelengths (yellow, orange, red) but the violet and blue light is [scattered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering "Rayleigh scattering") more, leaving light that is perceived as green.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-242)

Religious aspects
-----------------

Solar deities play a major role in many world religions and mythologies.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-APLLondon-243)[Worship of the Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_worship "Sun worship") was central to civilisations such as the [ancient Egyptians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt "Ancient Egypt"), the [Inca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inca "Inca") of South America and the [Aztecs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztec "Aztec") of what is now Mexico. In religions such as Hinduism, the Sun is still considered a god, known as [Surya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surya "Surya"). Many ancient monuments were constructed with solar phenomena in mind; for example, stone [megaliths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megalith "Megalith") accurately mark the summer or winter [solstice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solstice "Solstice") (for example in [Nabta Playa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nabta_Playa "Nabta Playa"), Egypt; [Mnajdra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mnajdra "Mnajdra"), Malta; and [Stonehenge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stonehenge "Stonehenge"), England); [Newgrange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newgrange "Newgrange"), a prehistoric human-built mount in Ireland, was designed to detect the winter solstice; the pyramid of [El Castillo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Castillo,_Chichen_Itza "El Castillo, Chichen Itza") at [Chichén Itzá](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1 "Chichén Itzá") in Mexico is designed to cast shadows in the shape of serpents climbing the [pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid "Pyramid") at the vernal and autumnal [equinoxes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equinox "Equinox").[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-244)

The ancient [Sumerians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumer "Sumer") believed that the Sun was [Utu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utu "Utu"),[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-245)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Nemet1998-246) the god of justice and twin brother of [Inanna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inanna "Inanna"), the [Queen of Heaven](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queen_of_Heaven_(antiquity) "Queen of Heaven (antiquity)").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-245) Later, Utu was identified with the [East Semitic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Semitic "East Semitic") god [Shamash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shamash "Shamash").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-245)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Nemet1998-246) Utu was regarded as a helper-deity, who aided those in distress.[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-245)

[![Image 33: A painting of Ra and Nefertari](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_001.jpg/250px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_001.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_001.jpg)

Ra from the [tomb of Nefertari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tomb_of_Nefertari "Tomb of Nefertari"), 13th century BC

From at least the [Fourth Dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourth_Dynasty "Fourth Dynasty") of Ancient Egypt, the Sun was worshipped as the [god Ra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ra "Ra"), portrayed as a falcon-headed divinity surmounted by the solar disk. In the [New Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt "New Kingdom of Egypt") period, the Sun became identified with the [dung beetle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dung_beetle "Dung beetle"). In the form of the sun disc [Aten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aten "Aten"), the Sun had a brief resurgence during the [Amarna Period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amarna_Period "Amarna Period") when it again became the preeminent, if not only, divinity for the Pharaoh [Akhenaten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akhenaten "Akhenaten").[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-247)[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-248) The Egyptians portrayed the god Ra as being carried across the sky in a solar barque, accompanied by lesser gods, and to the Greeks, he was Helios, carried by a chariot drawn by fiery horses. From the reign of [Elagabalus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elagabalus "Elagabalus") in the [late Roman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire "Decline of the Roman Empire") the Sun's birthday was a holiday celebrated as [Sol Invictus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sol_Invictus "Sol Invictus") (literally 'Unconquered Sun') soon after the winter solstice. The Sun appears from Earth to revolve once a year along the [ecliptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic "Ecliptic") through the [zodiac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zodiac "Zodiac"), and so Greek astronomers categorised it as one of the seven [planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_planets "Classical planets") (from Greek _planetes_, 'wanderer'); the naming of the [days of the weeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Names_of_the_days_of_the_week "Names of the days of the week") after the seven planets dates to the [Roman era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire "Roman Empire").[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-oed-249)[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-almagest-250)[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-251)

In [Proto-Indo-European religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion "Proto-Indo-European religion"), the Sun was personified as the goddess [*Seh 2 ul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_deity "Sun deity").[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-252)[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-MALLORY129-253) Derivatives of this goddess in [Indo-European languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-European_languages "Indo-European languages") include the [Old Norse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_Norse "Old Norse")_[Sól](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%B3l\_(sun) "Sól (sun)")_, [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit")_[Surya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surya "Surya")_, [Gaulish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaulish_language "Gaulish language")_[Sulis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulis "Sulis")_, [Lithuanian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuanian_language "Lithuanian language")_[Saulė](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saul%C4%97 "Saulė")_, and [Slavic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavic_languages "Slavic languages")_Solntse_.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-MALLORY129-253) In [ancient Greek religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion "Ancient Greek religion"), the sun deity was the male god Helios,[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-254) who in later times was [syncretised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syncretism "Syncretism") with [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo "Apollo").[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-255)

In ancient Roman culture, [Sunday](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunday "Sunday") was the day of the sun god. In paganism, the Sun was a source of life. It was the centre of a popular cult among Romans, who would stand at dawn to catch the first rays of sunshine as they prayed. The celebration of the [winter solstice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter_solstice "Winter solstice") (which influenced Christmas) was part of the Roman cult of Sol Invictus. It was adopted as the [Sabbath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabbath "Sabbath") day by Christians. The symbol of light was a pagan device adopted by Christians, and perhaps the most important one that did not come from Jewish traditions. Christian churches were built so that the congregation faced toward the sunrise.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-256) In the [Bible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bible "Bible"), the [Book of Malachi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_of_Malachi "Book of Malachi") mentions the "Sun of Righteousness", which some Christians have interpreted as a reference to the [Messiah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messiah "Messiah") ([Christ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christ "Christ")).[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-257)

[Tonatiuh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tonatiuh "Tonatiuh"), the Aztec god of the sun,[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Townsend1979-258) was closely associated with [human sacrifice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_sacrifice "Human sacrifice").[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Townsend1979-258) The sun goddess [Amaterasu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amaterasu "Amaterasu") is the most important deity in the [Shinto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shinto "Shinto") religion,[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Roberts_110-259)[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-260) and she is believed to be the direct ancestor of all [Japanese emperors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Emperors_of_Japan "List of Emperors of Japan").[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_note-Roberts_110-259)

See also
--------

*   [Advanced Composition Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Composition_Explorer "Advanced Composition Explorer")– NASA satellite of the Explorer program, at SE-L1 from 1997
*   [Analemma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analemma "Analemma")– Diagrammatic representation of Sun's position over a period of time
*   [Antisolar point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antisolar_point "Antisolar point")– Point on the celestial sphere opposite Sun
*   [Faint young Sun paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox "Faint young Sun paradox")– Paradox concerning water on early Earth
*   [List of brightest stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_brightest_stars "List of brightest stars")– Stars sorted by apparent magnitude
*   [List of nearest stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars "List of nearest stars")
*   [Midnight sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midnight_sun "Midnight sun")– Natural phenomenon when daylight lasts for a whole day
*   [Planets in astrology §Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planets_in_astrology#Sun "Planets in astrology")
*   [Solar telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_telescope "Solar telescope")– Telescope used to observe the Sun
*   [Sun path](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_path "Sun path")– Arc-like path that the Sun appears to follow across the sky
*   [Sun-Earth Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun-Earth_Day "Sun-Earth Day")– NASA and ESA joint educational program
*   [Sun in fiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_in_fiction "Sun in fiction")
*   [Timeline of the far future](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future "Timeline of the far future")– Scientific projections regarding the far future

Notes
-----

1.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-short_17-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-short_17-1)All numbers in this article are [short scale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Short_scale "Short scale"). One billion is 10 9, or 1,000,000,000.
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-heavy_elements_31-0)** In [astronomical sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy"), the term _heavy elements_ (or _metals_) refers to all chemical elements except hydrogen and helium.
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-40)**[Hydrothermal vent communities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent_communities "Hydrothermal vent communities") live so deep under the sea that they have no access to sunlight. Bacteria instead use sulfur compounds as an energy source, via [chemosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemosynthesis "Chemosynthesis").
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-rotation_51-0)** Counterclockwise is also the direction of revolution around the Sun for objects in the Solar System and is the direction of axial spin for most objects.
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-particle_density_93-0)** Earth's atmosphere near sea level has a particle density of about 2×10 25 m−3.

234.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun#cite_ref-Macdonald_239-0)**Macdonald, Lee (2012). "Equipment for Observing the Sun". _How to Observe the Sun Safely_. Patrick Moore's Practical Astronomy Series. New York: Springer. p.17. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1007/978-1-4614-3825-0_2](https://doi.org/10.1007%2F978-1-4614-3825-0_2). [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)")[978-1-4614-3824-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4614-3824-3 "Special:BookSources/978-1-4614-3824-3"). Never look directly at the Sun through any form of optical equipment, even for an instant. A brief glimpse of the Sun through a telescope is enough to cause permanent eye damage, or even blindness. Even looking at the Sun with the naked eye for more than a second or two is not safe. Do not assume that it is safe to look at the Sun through a filter, no matter how dark the filter appears to be.
